<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_environment_during_the_latest_Pleistocene">The environment during the latest Pleistocene</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: The environment during the latest Pleistocene">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>For an introduction to the radiocarbon dating techniques used by archaeologists and geologists, see <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a>.</i></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_and_submergence_of_Beringia">Emergence and submergence of Beringia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Emergence and submergence of Beringia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="700" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif/400px-Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif/600px-Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beringia_land_bridge-noaagov.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Figure1. Submergence of the Beringian land bridge with post-Last Glacial Maximum (LGM) rise in eustatic sea level</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Glaciation" title="Wisconsin Glaciation">Wisconsin Glaciation</a>, varying portions of the Earth's water were stored as glacier ice. As water accumulated in glaciers, the volume of water in the oceans correspondingly decreased, resulting in lowering of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eustatic_sea_level" title="Eustatic sea level">eustatic sea level</a>. The variation of the eustatic sea level with time has been reconstructed using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_isotope" title="Oxygen isotope">oxygen isotope</a> analysis of deep sea cores, the dating of marine terraces, and high resolution oxygen isotope sampling from ocean basins and modern ice caps. A drop of eustatic sea level by about 60 m to 120 m lower than present-day levels, commencing around 30,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>, caused the revealing of <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a> as a durable and extensive geographic feature connecting Siberia with Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup> With the rise of sea level after the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> (LGM), the Beringian land bridge was again submerged. Estimates of the final re-submergence of the Beringian land bridge based purely on present bathymetry of the Bering Strait and eustatic sea level curve place the event around 11,000 years BP (Figure 1). Ongoing research reconstructing Beringian paleogeography during deglaciation could change that estimate and possible earlier submergence could further constrain models of human migration into North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers">Glaciers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Glaciers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The onset of the Last Glacial Maximum after 30,000 years BP saw the expansion of alpine glaciers and continental ice sheets that blocked migration routes out of Beringia. By 21,000 years BP, and possibly thousands of years earlier, the <a href="/wiki/Cordilleran_Ice_Sheet" title="Cordilleran Ice Sheet">Cordilleran</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurentide_ice_sheet" title="Laurentide ice sheet">Laurentide</a> ice sheets coalesced east of the Rocky Mountains, closing off a potential migration route into the center of North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacksonetal1997_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacksonetal1997-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> Alpine glaciers in the coastal ranges and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaskan_Peninsula" title="Alaskan Peninsula">Alaskan Peninsula</a> isolated the interior of Beringia from the Pacific coast. Coastal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpine_glaciers" title="Alpine glaciers">alpine glaciers</a> and lobes of Cordilleran ice coalesced into piedmont glaciers that covered large stretches of the coastline as far south as <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Island" title="Vancouver Island">Vancouver Island</a> and formed an ice lobe across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straits_of_Juan_de_Fuca" title="Straits of Juan de Fuca">Straits of Juan de Fuca</a> by 15,000 <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating"><sup>14</sup>C years BP</a> (18,000 <a href="/wiki/Calibration_of_radiocarbon_dates" title="Calibration of radiocarbon dates">cal years</a> BP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyke2003_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dyke2003-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boothetal2003_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boothetal2003-9">[9]</a></sup> Coastal alpine glaciers started to retreat around 19,000 <a href="/wiki/Calibration_of_radiocarbon_dates" title="Calibration of radiocarbon dates">cal years</a> BP <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaiseetal1990_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blaiseetal1990-10">[10]</a></sup> while Cordilleran ice continued advancing in the <a href="/wiki/Puget_Sound_region" title="Puget Sound region">Puget lowlands</a> up to 14,000 <sup>14</sup>C years BP (16,800 cal years BP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boothetal2003_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boothetal2003-9">[9]</a></sup> Even during the maximum extent of coastal ice, unglaciated refugia persisted on present-day islands, that supported terrestrial and marine mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> As deglaciation occurred, refugia expanded until the coast became ice-free by 15,000 cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> The retreat of glaciers on the Alaskan Peninsula provided access from Beringia to the Pacific coast by around 17,000 cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Misartietal2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Misartietal2012-11">[11]</a></sup> The ice barrier between interior Alaska and the Pacific coast broke up starting around 13,500 <sup>14</sup>C years (16,200 cal years) BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyke2003_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dyke2003-8">[8]</a></sup> The ice-free corridor to the interior of North America opened between 13,000 and 12,000 cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacksonetal1997_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacksonetal1997-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> Glaciation in eastern Siberia during the LGM was limited to alpine and valley glaciers in mountain ranges and did not block access between Siberia and Beringia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_and_biological_environments">Climate and biological environments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Climate and biological environments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The paleoclimates and vegetation of eastern Siberia and Alaska during the Wisconsin glaciation have been deduced from high resolution oxygen isotope data and <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">stratigraphy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clagueetal2004_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clagueetal2004-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup> Prior to the Last Glacial Maximum, climates in eastern Siberia fluctuated between conditions approximating present day conditions and colder periods. The pre-LGM warm cycles in Arctic Siberia saw flourishes of megafaunas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup> The oxygen isotope record from the Greenland Ice Cap suggests that these cycles after about 45k years BP lasted anywhere from hundreds to between one and two thousand years, with greater duration of cold periods starting around 32k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup> The pollen record from Elikchan Lake, north of the Sea of Okhotsk, shows a marked shift from tree and shrub pollen to herb pollen prior to 26k <sup>14</sup>C years BP, as herb tundra replaced boreal forest and shrub steppe going into the LGM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup> A similar record of tree/shrub pollen being replaced with herb pollen as the LGM approached was recovered near the Kolyma River in Arctic Siberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup> The abandonment of the northern regions of Siberia due to rapid cooling or the retreat of game species with the onset of the LGM has been proposed to explain the lack of archaeosites in that region dating to the LGM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf2008_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf2008-14">[14]</a></sup> The pollen record from the Alaskan side shows shifts between herb/shrub and shrub tundra prior to the LGM, suggesting less dramatic warming episodes than those that allowed forest colonization on the Siberian side. Diverse, though not necessarily plentiful, megafaunas were present in those environments. Herb tundra dominated during the LGM, due to cold and dry conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clagueetal2004_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clagueetal2004-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coastal environments during the Last Glacial Maximum were complex. The lowered sea level, and an <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostatic</a> bulge equilibrated with the depression beneath the Cordilleran Ice Sheet, exposed the continental shelf to form a coastal plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedjeetal2004_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fedjeetal2004-15">[15]</a></sup> While much of the coastal plain was covered with piedmont glaciers, unglaciated refugia supporting terrestrial mammals have been identified on <a href="/wiki/Haida_Gwaii" title="Haida Gwaii">Haida Gwaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales_Island_(Alaska)" title="Prince of Wales Island (Alaska)">Prince of Wales Island</a>, and outer islands of the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Archipelago" title="Alexander Archipelago">Alexander Archipelago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clagueetal2004_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clagueetal2004-12">[12]</a></sup> The now-submerged coastal plain has potential for more refugia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clagueetal2004_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clagueetal2004-12">[12]</a></sup> Pollen data indicate mostly herb/shrub tundra vegetation in unglaciated areas, with some boreal forest towards the southern end of the range of Cordilleran ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clagueetal2004_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Clagueetal2004-12">[12]</a></sup> The coastal marine environment remained productive, as indicated by fossils of <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">pinnipeds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedjeetal2004_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fedjeetal2004-15">[15]</a></sup> The highly productive kelp forests over rocky marine shallows may have been a lure for coastal migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandsonetal2007_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandsonetal2007-17">[17]</a></sup> Reconstruction of the southern Beringian coastline also suggests potential for a highly productive coastal marine environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandsonetal2007_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandsonetal2007-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_changes_during_deglaciation">Environmental changes during deglaciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Environmental changes during deglaciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pollen data indicate a warm period culminating between 14k and 11k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (17k-13k cal years BP) followed by cooling between 11k-10k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (13k-11.5k cal years BP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedjeetal2004_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fedjeetal2004-15">[15]</a></sup> Coastal areas deglaciated rapidly as coastal alpine glaciers, then lobes of Cordilleran ice, retreated. The retreat was accelerated as sea levels rose and floated glacial termini. Estimates of a fully ice-free coast range between 16k<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedjeetal2004_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fedjeetal2004-15">[15]</a></sup> and 15k<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> cal years BP. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">Littoral</a> marine organisms colonized shorelines as ocean water replaced glacial meltwater. Replacement of herb/shrub tundra by coniferous forests was underway by 12.4k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (15k cal years BP) north of Haida Gwaii. Eustatic sea level rise caused flooding, which accelerated as the rate grew more rapid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedjeetal2004_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fedjeetal2004-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The inland Cordilleran and Laurentide ice sheets retreated more slowly than did the coastal glaciers. Opening of an ice-free corridor did not occur until after 13k to 12k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacksonetal1997_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jacksonetal1997-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> The early environment of the ice-free corridor was dominated by glacial outwash and meltwater, with ice-dammed lakes and periodic flooding from the release of ice-dammed meltwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup> Biological productivity of the deglaciated landscape was gained slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> The earliest possible viability of the ice-free corridor as a human migration route has been estimated at 11.5k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Birch forests were advancing across former herb tundra in Beringia by 14.3ka <sup>14</sup>C years BP (17k cal years BP) in response to climatic amelioration, indicating increased productivity of the landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology_and_sources_of_migration">Chronology and sources of migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chronology and sources of migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While most of the archaeological community is in general agreement that the Americas were settled by migrants from northeastern Asian populations, the chronology of the migrations, the source populations that contributed to the migrations, and their migration routes remain uncertain. The uncertainty is fed by a lack of archaeological evidence along migration routes that date to the periods when those migrations are proposed to have occurred; uncertainties in the dating and interpretation of the oldest proposed archaeosites in the Americas; and uncertainties of assumptions underlying chronological and source models of migration derived from studies of modern Native American genetics.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chronology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="536" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg/220px-Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg/330px-Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg/440px-Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Americas showing pre Clovis sites.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 21st century, the models of the chronology of migration are divided into two general approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White2006_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-White2006-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-19">[19]</a></sup> The first is the <i>short chronology theory</i>, that the first migration occurred after the Last Glacial Maximum, which went into decline after about 19,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaiseetal1990_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blaiseetal1990-10">[10]</a></sup> and was then followed by successive waves of immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The second theory is the <i>long chronology theory,</i> which proposes that the first group of people entered the Americas at a much earlier date, possibly 21,000–40,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonattoetal2009_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonattoetal2009-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinq1979_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cinq1979-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonnichsen1978_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonnichsen1978-23">[23]</a></sup> followed by a much later second wave of immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradshaw_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw-24">[24]</a></sup> Further controversy has been generated as age-dating of archaeosites in the Americas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodyear2005_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear2005-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay2000_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay2000-26">[26]</a></sup> and the timing of the opening of the ice-free corridor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacksonetal1997_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jacksonetal1997-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> have challenged the <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture#Clovis_First_.2F_Single_origin_hypothesis" title="Clovis culture">Clovis First theory</a>, which dominated thinking on New World anthropology for much of the 20th century.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_evidence">Archaeological evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Archaeological evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg"><img alt="Schematic illustration of maternal geneflow in and out of Beringia.Colours of the arrows correspond to approximate timing of the events and are decoded in the coloured time-bar. The initial peopling of Berinigia (depicted in light yellow) was followed by a standstill after which the ancestors of indigenous Americans spread swiftly all over the New World, while some of the Beringian maternal lineages–C1a-spread westwards. More recent (shown in green) genetic exchange is manifested by back-migration of A2a into Siberia and the spread of D2a into north-eastern America that post-dated the initial peopling of the New World." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="708" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/270px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/405px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/540px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg 2x" width="270"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Figure 2. Schematic illustration of maternal (mtDNA) gene-flow in and out of Beringia (long chronology, single source model).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png"><img alt='"Maps depicting each phase of the three-step early human migrations for the peopling of the Americas. (A) Gradual population expansion of the Amerind ancestors from their East Central Asian gene pool (blue arrow). (B) Proto-Amerind occupation of Beringia with little to no population growth for ≈20,000 years. (C) Rapid colonization of the New World by a founder group migrating southward through the ice-free, inland corridor between the eastern Laurentide and western Cordilleran Ice Sheets (green arrow) and/or along the Pacific coast (red arrow). In (B), the exposed seafloor is shown at its greatest extent during the last glacial maximum at ≈20–18 kya [25]. In (A) and (C), the exposed seafloor is depicted at ≈40 kya and ≈16 kya, when prehistoric sea levels were comparable. A scaled-down version of Beringia today (60% reduction of A–C) is presented in the lower left corner. This smaller map highlights the Bering Strait that has geographically separated the New World from Asia since ≈11–10 kya."' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1651" data-file-width="996" height="448" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png/270px-Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png/405px-Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png/540px-Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png 2x" width="270"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0001596.g004.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Figure 3. Illustration of hypothetical chronology for migration through Beringia (long chronology model). Not authoritative on timing of glacial features and not constrained by archaeological data.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pre-Last Glacial Maximum migration across Beringia into the Americas has been proposed to explain purported pre-LGM ages of archaeosites in the Americas such as <a href="/wiki/Bluefish_Caves" title="Bluefish Caves">Bluefish Caves</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinq1979_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cinq1979-22">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Crow_Flats" title="Old Crow Flats">Old Crow Flats</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonnichsen1978_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bonnichsen1978-23">[23]</a></sup> in the Yukon Territory, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meadowcroft_Rock_Shelter" title="Meadowcroft Rock Shelter">Meadowcroft Rock Shelter</a> in Pennsylvania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradshaw_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw-24">[24]</a></sup> The earlier <sup>14</sup>C date from a bone artifact at the Old Crow Flats site has been supplanted by an Accelerator Mass Spectrometry <sup>14</sup>C date that indicates a <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoebelBuvit2011_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoebelBuvit2011-27">[27]</a></sup> The interpretations of butcher marks and the geologic association of bones at the Bluefish Cave and Old Crow Flats sites have been called into question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon.2C_Guy_E_1998_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon.2C_Guy_E_1998-28">[28]</a></sup> The ages of the earliest positively identified artifacts at the Meadowcroft site are constrained by a compiled age estimate from <sup>14</sup>C in the range of 12k-15k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (13.8k-18.5k cal years BP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodyear2005_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear2005-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adovasioetal1990_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adovasioetal1990-29">[29]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Meadowcroft_Rockshelter" title="Meadowcroft Rockshelter">Meadowcroft Rockshelter</a> site and the <a href="/wiki/Monte_Verde" title="Monte Verde">Monte Verde</a> site in southern Chile, with a date of 14.8k cal years BP,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay2000_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay2000-26">[26]</a></sup> are the archaeosites in the Americas with the oldest dates that have gained broad acceptance.</p>
<p>Stones described as probable tools, <a href="/wiki/Hammerstone" title="Hammerstone">hammerstones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anvil" title="Anvil">anvils</a>, have been found in southern California, at the <a href="/wiki/Cerutti_Mastodon_site" title="Cerutti Mastodon site">Cerutti Mastodon site</a>, that are associated with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammut_americanum" title="Mammut americanum">mastodon</a> skeleton which appeared to have been processed by humans. The mastodon skeleton was dated by thorium-230/uranium radiometric analysis, using diffusion–adsorption–decay dating models, to 130.7 ± 9.4 thousand years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> No human bones were found, and the claims of tools and bone processing have been described as "not plausible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rincon_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rincon-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Waters" title="Michael R. Waters">Michael R. Waters</a> commented that "To demonstrate such early occupation of the Americas requires the presence of unequivocal stone artefacts. There are no unequivocal stone tools associated with the bones... this site is likely just an interesting paleontological locality." <a href="/wiki/Chris_Stringer" title="Chris Stringer">Chris Stringer</a> said that "extraordinary claims require extraordinary evidence - each aspect requires the strongest scrutiny," adding that "High and concentrated forces must have been required to smash the thickest mastodon bones, and the low energy depositional environment seemingly provides no obvious alternative to humans using the heavy cobbles found with the bones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rincon_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rincon-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yana_River" title="Yana River">Yana River</a> Rhino Horn site (RHS) has dated human occupation of eastern Arctic Siberia to 27k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (31.3k cal years BP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitulkoetal2004_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitulkoetal2004-32">[32]</a></sup> That date has been interpreted by some as evidence that migration into Beringia was imminent, lending credence to occupation of Beringia during the LGM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitchetenal2008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchetenal2008-34">[34]</a></sup> However, the Yana RHS date is from the beginning of the cooling period that led into the LGM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brighametal2004_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Brighametal2004-4">[4]</a></sup> But, a compilation of archaeosite dates throughout eastern Siberia suggest that the cooling period caused a retreat of humans southwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf2008_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graf2008-14">[14]</a></sup> Pre-LGM lithic evidence in Siberia indicate a settled lifestyle that was based on local resources, while post-LGM lithic evidence indicate a more migratory lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf2008_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graf2008-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The oldest archaeosite on the Alaskan side of Beringia date to 12k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (14k cal years BP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoebelBuvit2011_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-GoebelBuvit2011-27">[27]</a></sup> It is possible that a small founder population had entered Beringia before that time. However, archaeosites that date closer to the Last Glacial Maximum on either the Siberian or the Alaskan side of Beringia are lacking.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genomic_age_estimates">Genomic age estimates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Genomic age estimates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas">Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas</a>.</div>
<p>Recent studies of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas">Amerindian genetics</a> have used high resolution analytical techniques applied to DNA samples from modern Native Americans and Asian populations regarded as their source populations to reconstruct the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroups" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroups">human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroups</a> (yDNA <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup" title="Haplogroup">haplogroups</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroups" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroups">human mitochondrial DNA haplogroups</a> (mtDNA haplogroups) characteristic of Native American populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonattoetal2009_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bonattoetal2009-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitchetenal2008_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchetenal2008-34">[34]</a></sup> Models of molecular evolution rates are used to estimate the ages at which Native American DNA lineages branched off from their parent lineages in Asia and to deduce the ages of demographic events. One model based on Native American mtDNA Haplotypes (Figure 2) proposes that migration into <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a> occurred between 30k and 25k cal years BP, with migration into the Americas occurring around 10k to 15k years after isolation of the small <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founding population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup> Another model (Figure 3) proposes that migration into Beringia occurred approximately 36k cal years BP, followed by 20k years of isolation in Beringia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitchetenal2008_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchetenal2008-34">[34]</a></sup> Yet another model proposes that migration into Beringia occurred between 40k and 30k cal years BP, with a pre-LGM migration into the Americas followed by isolation of the northern population following closure of the ice-free corridor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonattoetal2009_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bonattoetal2009-21">[21]</a></sup> The three Native American mtDNA evolution rate models all fall within the long chronology theory of migration into the Americas.</p>
<p>A study of the diversification of mtDNA Haplogroups C and D from southern Siberia and eastern Asia, respectively, suggests that the parent lineage (Subhaplogroup D4h) of Subhaplogroup D4h3, a lineage found among Native Americans and Han Chinese,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kempetal2007_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kempetal2007-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peregoetal2009_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peregoetal2009-36">[36]</a></sup> emerged around 20k cal years BP, constraining the emergence of D4h3 to post-LGM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Derenkoetal2010_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Derenkoetal2010-37">[37]</a></sup> Age estimates based on Y-chromosome micro-satellite diversity place origin of the American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_Q1a3a_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup Q1a3a (Y-DNA)">Haplogroup Q1a3a (Y-DNA)</a> at around 10k to 15k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bortolinietal2003_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bortolinietal2003-38">[38]</a></sup> Greater consistency of DNA molecular evolution rate models with each other and with archaeological data may be gained by the use of dated fossil DNA to calibrate molecular evolution rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kempetal2007_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kempetal2007-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_populations">Source populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Source populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is general agreement among anthropologists that the source populations for the migration into the Americas originated from an area somewhere east of the <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei River</a>. The common occurrence of the mtDNA Haplogroups A, B, C, and D among eastern Asian and Native American populations has long been recognized, along with the presence of Haplogroup X.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schurr2000_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schurr2000-39">[39]</a></sup> As a whole, the greatest frequency of the four Native American associated haplogroups occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baikal" title="Baikal">Baikal</a> region of southern Siberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zakharovetal2004_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zakharovetal2004-40">[40]</a></sup> Some subclades of C and D closer to the Native American subclades occur among Mongolian, Amur, Japanese, Korean, and Ainu populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schurr2000_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schurr2000-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starikovskayaetal2005_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starikovskayaetal2005-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_genomic_models">Human genomic models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Human genomic models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a non-technical introduction to genetics in general, see <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_genetics" title="Introduction to genetics">Introduction to genetics</a>.</div>
<p>The development of high-resolution genomic analysis has provided opportunities to further define Native American subclades and narrow the range of Asian subclades that may be parent or sister subclades. For example, the broad geographic range of Haplogroup X has been interpreted as allowing the possibility of a western Eurasian, or even a European source population for Native Americans, as in the <a href="/wiki/Solutrean_hypothesis" title="Solutrean hypothesis">Solutrean hypothesis</a>, or suggesting a pre-Last Glacial Maximum migration into the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schurr2000_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schurr2000-39">[39]</a></sup> The analysis of an ancient variant of Haplogroup X among aboriginals of the Altai region indicates common ancestry with the European strain rather than descent from the European strain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zakharovetal2004_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zakharovetal2004-40">[40]</a></sup> Further division of X subclades has allowed identification of Subhaplogroup X2a, which is regarded as specific to Native Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peregoetal2009_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peregoetal2009-36">[36]</a></sup> With further definition of subclades related to Native American populations, the requirements for sampling Asian populations to find the most closely related subclades grow more specific. Subhaplogroups D1 and D4h3 have been regarded as Native American specific based on their absence among a large sampling of populations regarded as potential descendants of source populations, over a wide area of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup> Among the 3764 samples, the <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> - lower <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">Amur</a> region was represented by 61 <a href="/wiki/Orok_people" title="Orok people">Oroks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup> In another study, Subhaplogroup D1a has been identified among the <a href="/wiki/Ulch_people" title="Ulch people">Ulchis</a> of the lower Amur River region(4 among 87 sampled, or 4.6%), along with Subhaplogroup C1a (1 among 87, or 1.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starikovskayaetal2005_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Starikovskayaetal2005-41">[41]</a></sup> Subhaplogroup C1a is regarded as a close sister clade of the Native American Subhaplogroup C1b.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starikovskayaetal2005_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Starikovskayaetal2005-41">[41]</a></sup> Subhaplogroup D1a has also been found among ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon" title="Jōmon">Jōmon</a> skeletons from <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adachietal2009_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adachietal2009-42">[42]</a></sup> The modern <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a> are regarded as descendants of the Jōmon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adachietal2009_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adachietal2009-42">[42]</a></sup> The occurrence of the Subhaplogroups D1a and C1a in the lower Amur region suggests a source population from that region distinct from the Altai-Baikal source populations, where sampling did not reveal those two particular subclades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starikovskayaetal2005_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Starikovskayaetal2005-41">[41]</a></sup> The conclusions regarding Subhaplogroup D1 indicating potential source populations in the lower Amur<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starikovskayaetal2005_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-Starikovskayaetal2005-41">[41]</a></sup> and Hokkaido<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adachietal2009_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adachietal2009-42">[42]</a></sup> areas stand in contrast to the single-source migration model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonattoetal2009_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bonattoetal2009-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tammetal2007_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Tammetal2007-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitchetenal2008_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchetenal2008-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Subhaplogroup D4h3 has been identified among <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kempetal2007_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kempetal2007-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peregoetal2009_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peregoetal2009-36">[36]</a></sup> Subhaplogroup D4h3 from China does not have the same geographic implication as Subhaplotype D1a from Amur-Hokkaido, so its implications for source models are more speculative. Its parent lineage, Subhaplotype D4h, is believed to have emerged in east Asia, rather than Siberia, around 20k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Derenkoetal2010_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Derenkoetal2010-37">[37]</a></sup> Subhaplogroup D4h2, a sister clade of D4h3, has also been found among Jōmon skeletons from Hokkaido.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adachietal2011_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adachietal2011-43">[43]</a></sup> D4h3 has a coastal trace in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peregoetal2009_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Peregoetal2009-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The contrast between the genetic profiles of the Hokkaido Jōmon skeletons and the modern Ainu illustrates another uncertainty in source models derived from modern DNA samples:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adachietal2009_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Adachietal2009-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>However, probably due to the small sample size or close consanguinity among the members of the site, the frequencies of the haplogroups in Funadomari skeletons were quite different from any modern populations, including Hokkaido Ainu, who have been regarded as the direct descendant of the Hokkaido Jomon people.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The descendants of source populations with the closest relationship to the genetic profile from the time when differentiation occurred are not obvious. Source population models can be expected to become more robust as more results are compiled, the heritage of modern proxy candidates becomes better understood, and fossil DNA in the regions of interest is found and considered.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="HTLV-1_genomics">HTLV-1 genomics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: HTLV-1 genomics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Human T cell Lymphotrophic Virus 1 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HTLV-1" title="HTLV-1">HTLV-1</a>) is a virus transmitted through exchange of bodily fluids and from mother to child through breast milk. The mother-to-child transmission mimics a hereditary trait, although such transmission from maternal carriers is less than 100%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lietal2004_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lietal2004-44">[44]</a></sup> The HTLV virus genome has been mapped, allowing identification of four major strains and analysis of their antiquity through mutations. The highest geographic concentrations of the strain HLTV-1 are in sub-Saharan Africa and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verdoncketal2007_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verdoncketal2007-45">[45]</a></sup> In Japan, it occurs in its highest concentration on <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verdoncketal2007_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Verdoncketal2007-45">[45]</a></sup> It is also present among African descendants and native populations in the Caribbean region and South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verdoncketal2007_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Verdoncketal2007-45">[45]</a></sup> It is rare in Central America and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verdoncketal2007_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Verdoncketal2007-45">[45]</a></sup> Its distribution in the Americas has been regarded as due to importation with the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gessainetal1992_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gessainetal1992-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ainu have developed antibodies to HTLV-1, indicating its endemicity to the Ainu and its antiquity in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ishidaetal1985_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ishidaetal1985-47">[47]</a></sup> A subtype "A" has been defined and identified among the Japanese (including Ainu), and among Caribbean and South American isolates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miuraeta1994_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miuraeta1994-48">[48]</a></sup> A subtype "B" has been identified in Japan and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miuraeta1994_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miuraeta1994-48">[48]</a></sup> In 1995, Native Americans in coastal British Columbia were found to have both subtypes A and B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Picardetal1995_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Picardetal1995-49">[49]</a></sup> Bone marrow specimens from an Andean mummy about 1500 years old were reported to have shown the presence of the A subtype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lietal1999_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lietal1999-50">[50]</a></sup> The finding ignited controversy, with contention that the sample DNA was insufficiently complete for the conclusion and that the result reflected modern contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulthart2000_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulthart2000-51">[51]</a></sup> However, a re-analysis indicated that the DNA sequences were consistent with, but not definitely from, the "cosmopolitan clade" (subtype A).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulthart2000_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coulthart2000-51">[51]</a></sup> The presence of subtypes A and B in the Americas is suggestive of a Native American source population related to the Ainu ancestors, the Jōmon.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_anthropology">Physical anthropology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Physical anthropology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Paleoamerican skeletons in the Americas such as <a href="/wiki/Kennewick_Man" title="Kennewick Man">Kennewick Man</a> (Washington State), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoya_Negro_skeleton" title="Hoya Negro skeleton">Hoya Negro skeleton</a> (Yucatán), <a href="/wiki/Luzia_Woman" title="Luzia Woman">Luzia Woman</a> and other skulls from the <a href="/wiki/Lagoa_Santa,_Minas_Gerais" title="Lagoa Santa, Minas Gerais">Lagoa Santa</a> site (Brazil), <a href="/wiki/Buhl_Woman" title="Buhl Woman">Buhl Woman</a> (Idaho), <a href="/wiki/Pe%C3%B1on_woman" title="Peñon woman">Peñon Woman III</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalezetal2015_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalezetal2015-52">[52]</a></sup> two skulls from the Tlapacoya site (Mexico City),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalezetal2015_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalezetal2015-52">[52]</a></sup> and 33 skulls from Baja California<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GonzalezJetal2003_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-GonzalezJetal2003-53">[53]</a></sup> have exhibited craniofacial traits distinct from most modern Native Americans, leading physical anthropologists to the opinion that some Paleoamericans were of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australoid" title="Australoid">Australoid</a> rather than Siberian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay2003_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay2003-54">[54]</a></sup> The most basic measured distinguishing trait is the <a href="/wiki/Cephalic_index" title="Cephalic index">dolichocephaly</a> of the skull. Some modern isolates such as the <a href="/wiki/Peric%C3%BAes" title="Pericúes">Pericúes</a> of Baja California and the <a href="/wiki/Fuegians" title="Fuegians">Fuegians</a> of Tierra del Fuego exhibit that same morphological trait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GonzalezJetal2003_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-GonzalezJetal2003-53">[53]</a></sup> Other anthropologists advocate an alternative hypothesis that evolution of an original Beringian <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> gave rise to a distinct morphology that was similar in all known Paleoamerican skulls, followed by later convergence towards the modern Native American phenotype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiedel2004_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fiedel2004-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chattersetal2014_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chattersetal2014-56">[56]</a></sup> Resolution of the issue awaits the identification of a Beringian phenotype among paleoamerican skulls or evidence of a genetic clustering among examples of the Australoid phenotype.</p>
<p>A report published in the <i><a href="/wiki/American_Journal_of_Physical_Anthropology" title="American Journal of Physical Anthropology">American Journal of Physical Anthropology</a></i> in January 2015 reviewed craniofacial variation focussing on differences between early and late Native Americans and explanations for these based on either skull morphology or molecular genetics. Arguments based on molecular genetics have in the main, according to the authors, accepted a single migration from Asia with a probable pause in Berengia, plus later bi-directional gene flow. Studies focussing on craniofacial morphology have argued that Paleoamerican remains have "been described as much closer to African and Australo-Melanesians populations than to the modern series of Native Americans", suggesting two entries into the Americas, an early one occurring before a distinctive East Asian morphology developed (referred to in the paper as the "Two Components Model". A third model, the "Recurrent Gene Flow" [RGF] model, attempts to reconcile the two, arguing that circumarctic gene flow after the initial migration could account for morphological changes. It specifically re-evaluates the original report on the Hoya Negro skeleton which supported the RGF model, the authors disagreed with the original conclusion which suggested that the skull shape did not match those of modern Native Americans, arguing that the "skull falls into a subregion of the morphospace occupied by both Paleoamericans and some modern Native Americans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stemmed_points">Stemmed points</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Stemmed points">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Stemmed points are a lithic technology distinct from Beringian and Clovis types. They have a distribution ranging from coastal east Asia to the Pacific coast of South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup> The emergence of stemmed points has been traced to Korea during the upper Paleolithic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The origin and distribution of stemmed points have been interpreted as a cultural marker related to a source population from coastal east Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_routes">Migration routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Migration routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior_route">Interior route</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Interior route">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="724" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png/220px-Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png/330px-Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png/440px-Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peopling_of_America_through_Beringia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing the approximate location of the ice-free corridor and specific Paleoindian sites (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clovis_theory" title="Clovis theory">Clovis theory</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historically, theories about migration into the Americas have centered on migration from Beringia through the interior of North America. The discovery of artifacts in association with Pleistocene faunal remains near <a href="/wiki/Clovis,_New_Mexico" title="Clovis, New Mexico">Clovis, New Mexico</a> in the early 1930s required extension of the timeframe for the settlement of North America to the period during which glaciers were still extensive. That led to the hypothesis of a migration route between the Laurentide and Cordilleran ice sheets to explain the early settlement. The Clovis site was host to a lithic technology characterized by spear points with an indentation, or flute, where the point was attached to the shaft. A lithic complex characterized by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clovis_Point" title="Clovis Point">Clovis Point</a></i> technology was subsequently identified over much of North America and in South America. The association of Clovis complex technology with late Pleistocene faunal remains led to the theory that it marked the arrival of big game hunters that migrated out of Beringia then dispersed throughout the Americas, otherwise known as the Clovis First theory.</p>
<p>Recent radiocarbon dating of Clovis sites has yielded ages of 11.1k to 10.7k <sup>14</sup>C years BP (13k to 12.6k cal years BP), somewhat later than dates derived from older techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watersstafford2007_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-watersstafford2007-60">[60]</a></sup> The re-evaluation of earlier radiocarbon dates led to the conclusion that no fewer than 11 of the 22 Clovis sites with radiocarbon dates are "problematic" and should be disregarded, including the <a href="/wiki/Type_site" title="Type site">type site</a> in Clovis, New Mexico. Numerical dating of Clovis sites has allowed comparison of Clovis dates with dates of other archaeosites throughout the Americas, and of the opening of the ice-free corridor. Both lead to significant challenges to the Clovis First theory. The Monte Verde site of Southern Chile has been dated at 14.8k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay2000_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay2000-26">[26]</a></sup> The Paisley Cave site in eastern Oregon yielded a <sup>14</sup>C date of 12.4k years (14.5k cal years) BP, on a coprolite with human DNA and <sup>14</sup>C dates of 11.3k-11k (13.2k-12.9k cal years) BP on horizons containing western stemmed points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkinsetal2012_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkinsetal2012-61">[61]</a></sup> Artifact horizons with non-Clovis lithic assemblages and pre-Clovis ages occur in eastern North America, although the maximum ages tend to be poorly constrained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodyear2005_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear2005-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adovasioetal1990_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adovasioetal1990-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geological findings on the timing of the ice-free corridor also challenge the notion that Clovis and pre-Clovis human occupation of the Americas was a result of migration through that route following the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>. Pre-LGM closing of the corridor may approach 30k cal years BP and estimates of ice retreat from the corridor are in the range of 12 to 13k cal years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacksonetal1997_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jacksonetal1997-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> Viability of the corridor as a human migration route has been estimated at 11.5k cal years BP, later than the ages of the Clovis and pre-Clovis sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> Dated Clovis archaeosites suggest a south-to-north spread of the Clovis culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pre-Last Glacial Maximum migration into the interior has been proposed to explain pre-Clovis ages for archaeosites in the Americas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradshaw_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw-24">[24]</a></sup> although pre-Clovis sites such as Meadowcroft Rock Shelter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodyear2005_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Goodyear2005-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adovasioetal1990_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adovasioetal1990-29">[29]</a></sup> Monte Verde,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay2000_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay2000-26">[26]</a></sup> and Paisley Cave have not yielded confirmed pre-LGM ages.</p>
<p>The interior route is consistent with the spread of the Na Dene language group and Subhaplogroup X2a into the Americas after the earliest paleoamerican migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peregoetal2009_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Peregoetal2009-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific_coastal_route">Pacific coastal route</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pacific coastal route">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="3000" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png/340px-World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png/510px-World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png/680px-World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png 2x" width="340"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Map_of_Y-DNA_Haplogroups1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Possible migration routes to the Americas as predicted by the distribution of Y-DNA haplogroups: inland route (purple lines), Pacific coastal route (brown dashed line), and possible trans-Atlantic route (light blue double line).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">Coastal migration</a></div>
<p>Pacific models propose that people first reached the Americas via water travel, following coastlines from northeast Asia into the Americas. Coastlines are unusually productive environments because they provide humans with access to a diverse array of plants and animals from both terrestrial and marine ecosystems. While not exclusive of land-based migrations, the Pacific 'coastal migration theory' helps explain how early colonists reached areas extremely distant from the Bering Strait region, including sites such as <a href="/wiki/Monte_Verde" title="Monte Verde">Monte Verde</a> in southern Chile and <a href="/wiki/Taima-Taima" title="Taima-Taima">Taima-Taima</a> in western <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>. Two cultural components were discovered at Monte Verde near the Pacific coast of Chile. The youngest layer is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_carbon_dating" title="Radio carbon dating">radiocarbon dated</a> at 12,500 radiocarbon years (~14,000 cal BP)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and has produced the remains of several types of seaweeds collected from coastal habitats. The older and more controversial component may date back as far as 33,000 years, but few scholars currently accept this very early component.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>As the chronology of deglaciation in the interior and coastal regions of North America became better understood, the coastal migration hypothesis was advanced by Knute Fladmark as an alternative to the ice-free corridor hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Debate on coastal versus interior migration for initial settlement has centered on evidence for chronology of initial settlement of Beringia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasilevetal2002_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-Vasilevetal2002-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoebelBuvit2011_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-GoebelBuvit2011-27">[27]</a></sup> interior North America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup> the Pacific coast of the Americas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay2000_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay2000-26">[26]</a></sup> and timing of the opening of coastal versus interior migration routes indicated by geological evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geotimescorridor_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-Geotimescorridor-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> Complicating the debate has been the absence of archaeological data from the coastal and interior migration routes from the periods when the initial migration is proposed to have occurred. A recent variation of the coastal migration hypothesis is the marine migration hypothesis, which proposes that migrants with boats settled in coastal refugia during deglaciation of the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandsonetal2007_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandsonetal2007-17">[17]</a></sup> The proposed use of boats adds a measure of flexibility to the chronology of coastal migration, as a continuous ice-free coast (16k-15k cal years BP) would no longer be required. A coastal east Asian source population is integral to the marine migration hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandsonetal2007_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandsonetal2007-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, the autosomal DNA of a toddler from Montana, dated at 10.7k <sup>14</sup>C years (12.5-12.7 cal years) BP was sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rasmussen2014_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen2014-63">[63]</a></sup> The DNA was taken from a skeleton referred to as <a href="/wiki/Anzick-1" title="Anzick-1">Anzick-1</a>, found in close association with several Clovis artifacts. The analysis yielded identification of the mtDNA as belonging to Subhaplogroup D4h3a, a rare subclade of D4h3 occurring along the west coast of the Americas, as well as geneflow related to the Siberian Mal'ta population. The data indicate that Anzick-1 is from a population directly ancestral to present South American and Central American Native American populations. Anzick-1 is less closely related to present North American Native American populations. D4h3a has been identified as a clade associated with coastal migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peregoetal2009_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Peregoetal2009-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The problems associated with finding archaeological evidence for migration during a period of lowered sea level are well known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geotimesquest_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-geotimesquest-64">[64]</a></sup> Sites related to the first migration are usually submerged, so the location of such sites is obscured. Certain types of evidence dependent on organic material, such as radiocarbon dating, may be destroyed by submergence. Wave action can destroy site structures and scatter artifacts along a prograding shoreline. Additionally, Pacific coastal conditions tend to be unstable due to steep unstable terrain, earthquakes, tsunamis, and volcanoes. Strategies for finding earliest migration sites include identifying potential sites on submerged paleoshorelines, seeking sites in areas uplifted either by tectonics or isostatic rebound, and looking for riverine sites in areas that may have attracted coastal migrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlandson2011_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Erlandson2011-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geotimesquest_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-geotimesquest-64">[64]</a></sup> Otherwise, coastal archaeology is dependent on secondary evidence related to lifestyles and technologies of maritime peoples from sites similar to those that would be associated with the original migration.</p>
<p>Other coastal models, dealing specifically with the peopling of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> and California coasts, have been advocated by archaeologists Knut Fladmark, Roy Carlson, James Dixon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jon_Erlandson" title="Jon Erlandson">Jon Erlandson</a>, Ruth Gruhn, and Daryl Fedje. In a 2007 article in the <i>Journal of Island and Coastal Archaeology,</i> Erlandson and his colleagues proposed a corollary to the coastal migration theory—the "kelp highway hypothesis"—arguing that productive <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> forests supporting similar suites of plants and animals would have existed near the end of the Pleistocene around much of the Pacific Rim from Japan to Beringia, the Pacific Northwest, and California, as well as the Andean Coast of South America. Once the coastlines of Alaska and British Columbia had deglaciated about 16,000 years ago, these kelp forest (along with estuarine, mangrove, and coral reef) habitats would have provided an ecologically similar migration corridor, entirely at sea level, and essentially unobstructed.</p>
<p>A 2016 DNA analysis of plants and animals suggest a coastal route was feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asians:_Paleoindians_of_the_coast">East Asians: Paleoindians of the coast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: East Asians: Paleoindians of the coast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples#Genetic_studies" title="Austronesian peoples">Genetic studies of Austronesian peoples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples#Migration_and_dispersion" title="Austronesian peoples">Migration and dispersion of Austronesian peoples to the Americas supported by coconut and sweet potato population genetics</a></div>
<p>The boat-builders from <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a>) may have been one of the earliest groups to reach the shores of North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coco_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-coco-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> One theory suggests people in boats followed the coastline from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurile_Islands" title="Kurile Islands">Kurile Islands</a> to <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> down the coasts of North and South America as far as <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> 62<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandryketal2001_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Mandryketal2001-7">[7]</a></sup> 54, 57. The <a href="/wiki/Haida_people" title="Haida people">Haida</a> nation on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Charlotte_Islands" title="Queen Charlotte Islands">Queen Charlotte Islands</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> may have originated from these early Asian mariners between 25,000 and 12,000 years ago. Early watercraft migration would also explain the habitation of coastal sites in South America such as <a href="/wiki/Pikimachay" title="Pikimachay">Pikimachay Cave</a> in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by 20,000 years ago (disputed) and <a href="/wiki/Monte_Verde" title="Monte Verde">Monte Verde</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> by 13,000 years ago [6 30; 8 383].</p>
<blockquote>
<p>'There was boat use in Japan 20,000 years ago,' says <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jon_Erlandson" title="Jon Erlandson">Jon Erlandson</a>, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oregon" title="University of Oregon">University of Oregon</a> anthropologist. 'The Kurile Islands (north of Japan) are like stepping stones to Beringia,' the then continuous land bridging the Bering Strait. Migrants, he said, could have then skirted the tidewater glaciers in Canada right on down the coast. [7 64]'</p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_with_evaluating_coastal_migration_models">Problems with evaluating coastal migration models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Problems with evaluating coastal migration models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The coastal migration models provide a different perspective on migration to the New World, but they are not without their own problems. One of the biggest problems<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> is that global sea levels have risen over 120 metres (390 ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-sea-level_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-sea-level-70">[70]</a></sup> since the end of the last glacial period, and this has submerged the ancient coastlines that maritime people would have followed into the Americas. Finding sites associated with early coastal migrations is extremely difficult—and systematic excavation of any sites found in deeper waters is challenging and expensive. On the other hand, there is evidence of marine technologies found in the hills of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Channel_Islands_of_California" title="Channel Islands of California">Channel Islands of California</a>, circa 10,000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> If there was an early pre-Clovis coastal migration, there is always the possibility of a "failed colonization". Another problem that arises is the lack of hard evidence found for a "long chronology" theory. No sites have yet produced a consistent chronology older than about 12,500 radiocarbon years (~14,500 calendar years)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, but research has been limited in South America related to the possibility of early coastal migrations.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Y-DNA_among_South_American_and_Alaskan_natives">Y-DNA among South American and Alaskan natives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Y-DNA among South American and Alaskan natives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsatellite_(genetics)" title="Microsatellite (genetics)">micro-satellite</a> diversity and distribution of a Y lineage specific to South America suggest that certain Amerindian populations became isolated after the initial colonization of their regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subclades_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-subclades-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Na-Den%C3%A9" title="Na-Dené">Na-Dené</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Natives" title="Alaska Natives">Indigenous Alaskan</a> populations exhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_Q_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup Q (Y-DNA)">haplogroup Q (Y-DNA)</a> mutations, but are distinct from other indigenous Amerindians with various mtDNA and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autosomal_DNA" title="Autosomal DNA">autosomal DNA</a> (atDNA) mutations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NaDene_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-NaDene-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhivotovsky_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhivotovsky-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inuit_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-inuit-75">[75]</a></sup> This suggests that the earliest migrants into the northern extremes of North America and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> derived from later migrant populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadene1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nadene1-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_hypotheses">Other hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Other known or supposed trans-oceanic migrations and contacts are not within the scope of this article. The well-documented 10th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Norse colonization of the Americas">Viking colonies in Canada</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Solutrean_hypothesis" title="Solutrean hypothesis">Solutrean hypothesis</a> of Palaeolithic migration from Europe to the Americas, are discussed in those articles. An examination of the many theories, myths and legends about the settlement of the Americas is found in this article about <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories" title="Pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact theories">Pre-Columbian contact.</a></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Settlement_of_the_Americas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Notes</b></p>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">

<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RI32r548fUwC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;dq=The%20first%20Americans%3A%20the%20Pleistocene%20colonization%20of%20the%20New%20World%20%20By%20Nina%20G.%20Jablonski&amp;pg=PP1#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=true" rel="nofollow">The First Americans: The Pleistocene Colonization of the New World</a> - by Nina G. Jablonski - <a href="/wiki/California_Academy_of_Sciences" title="California Academy of Sciences">California Academy of Sciences</a>, 2002 <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-940228-49-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-940228-49-1">0-940228-49-1</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WAsKm-_zu5sC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;dq=The%20Journey%20of%20Man&amp;pg=PP1#v=onepage&amp;q=&amp;f=true" rel="nofollow">Journey of Man: A Genetic Odyssey (book)</a> - by <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Wells" title="Spencer Wells">Spencer Wells</a> - <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University_Press" title="Princeton University Press">Princeton University Press</a>, 2002 <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8129-7146-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-8129-7146-9">0-8129-7146-9</a></li>
</ul>
